<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/47/E7619/F7.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/534449" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 7.</span> <p id="p-46" class="first-child">Hierarchical clustering of the expression patterns of the members of the five gene families responsible for mogroside biosynthesis: <em>SE</em>, <em>EPH</em>, <em>CDS</em>, <em>CYPs</em>, and <em>UGTs</em>. The cluster containing the genes of the pathway is outlined in blue and is expanded to the right. The genes shown to be involved in mogroside biosynthesis are marked by an asterisk. An expandable version of the entire heat map is presented in <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S24</a> in which the additional genes identified in this paper are marked. Genes with numbers containing decimal points are derived from the UGT-containing scaffolds, and the number following the decimal indicates its position in the tandem arrangement. Genes with numbers containing a lower hyphen are derived from the CYP-containing scaffolds, and the last number indicates its position in the tandem arrangement. Genes beginning with the letter “S” are derived from the genomic scaffolds. All genes are described in <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sd02.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>. The <em>CYP87D18</em> coding for the C11-hydroxylating enzyme is listed as S623.1, closely clustering with the <em>CDS</em> gene.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-47">The role of UGT720-269–1 in mogroside synthesis is also supported by its gene expression pattern, which is broadly coordinated with those of the enzymes of mogrol synthesis. It is most highly expressed in the young fruit (15 d and 34 d) and declines with fruit maturity, although not as strikingly as the strict hierarchical cluster of early fruit expression (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S14 and S24</a>). This correlates as well with the developmental pattern of mogroside accumulation that indicated that the M2 product was already at its peak concentration in the early fruit. The expression patterns of the other C3 glucosylation genes of UGT74 and UGT75 did not show the pattern of early fruit expression, nor did the members of the UGT73 family (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S14 and S24</a>). Nevertheless, these functionally active genes were expressed in the young <em>Siraitia</em> fruit, and their involvement in mogroside synthesis cannot be ruled out.</p><p id="p-48">Only the branching enzyme family UGT94 exhibited a strikingly different expression pattern, strongly up-regulated in the latter stages of development (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S14 and S24</a>) in agreement with the accumulation of the branched mogrosides in the maturing fruit. This expression pattern is also novel to <em>Siraitia</em> fruit; however, the evolutionary changes in the expression of this enzyme family are unlikely to be causal to mogroside metabolism because it is temporally distinct from the committed early stages of mogroside M2 biosynthesis, when the novelty of mogroside accumulation is already determined.</p><p id="p-49">The possibility exists that, together with the coordinated expression of the mogroside pathway in the fruit, the defining characteristic of which is the <em>trans</em>-dihydroxy decorations at the 24,25 position, there is additional metabolic control on the flux of the pathway, perhaps specifically through an enhanced level of diepoxysqualene. The novelty of <em>Siraitia</em> fruit mogroside synthesis may also lie with metabolic compartmentation characteristics of the fruit. The <em>Siraitia</em> squalene epoxidase reaction drives the pathway to diepoxysqualene, perhaps by involving novel metabolic channeling (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">41</a>) or perhaps by the low levels of competing pathways for the initial squalene monoepoxide product. In support of the latter, the expression pattern of the single cycloartenol synthase (CAS) gene is temporally asynchronous with early mogroside accumulation, compared with CDS. CAS expression is about 20% the expression as that of CDS at the earliest stage of fruit development and increases dramatically only upon the sharp decline of CDS expression in the 34-DAA fruit (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S14</a>). Alternatively, the compartmentalization/subcellular localization of the pathway enzymes may contribute to the flux control, as it appears from analysis of predicted enzyme localization using numerous targeting programs that all of the enzymes may be localized to organelles, either ER or peroxisomes (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S15</a>). Further research is required to account for these components of control of the flux and accumulation of mogrosides.</p><p id="p-50">In conclusion, we have identified the and strikingly coordinated metabolic pathway for the natural, noncaloric sweetener mogroside. This insight is expected to facilitate the production of low-cost, high-intensity natural sweeteners.</p></div></div><div class="section methods" id="sec-13"><h2 class="">Methods</h2><div id="sec-14" class="subsection"><h3>Plant Material.</h3><p id="p-51">Seeds of <em>S. grosvenorii</em> were purchased from B &amp; T World Seeds, germinated under sterile conditions, and grown trellised under shade netting in Bet Dagan, Israel. Of nearly 30 plants, a single female plant produced a flush of flowers in May 2011 and, in the absence of male flowers, the newly opened female flowers were treated with a hormone spray (20 ppm naphthalenacetic acid, commercial formulation from Alphatop, Perelman Co.) to induce a parthenocarpic fruit set. Flowers were tagged and fruit was sampled over the following 103-d period, when the fruits had turned a ripe beige. In the laboratory, plant tissue was stored at −80 °C. Samples of at least three fruit at each stage were harvested at 15, 34, 55, 77, 90, and 103 DAA. Samples of mature leaves (most recent full-sized), stems, and root tissue were similarly prepared.</p></div><div id="sec-15" class="subsection"><h3>Chromatographic Separations and Identifications.</h3><p id="p-52">Details of metabolite extractions, chromatographic separations, standards preparation, and NMR analysis are provided in <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix, Methods</em></a>.</p></div><div id="sec-16" class="subsection"><h3>DNA Isolation, RNA Isolation, Library Preparation, and Sequencing.</h3><p id="p-53">See <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Methods</em></a>.</p></div><div id="sec-17" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Assembly.</h3><p id="p-54">Initial assembly of short 100-bp PE reads into 2,000-bp “super-reads”’ was carried out using the MaSuRCA‐2.1.0 Genome Assembler (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">42</a>). Following this, the super-reads were combined with the TLSR reads using Celera Assembler 8.2 (<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">43</a>) (assembly parameters and assembly statistics in <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S2</a>). Scaffolding was done with SSPACE (<a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">44</a>), combining the two additional mate-pair libraries (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S3</a>). Due to the single-strand origin of the TLSR sequences it is possible that alleles for the same locus are represented on different scaffolds, accounting for the high similarities especially among the CYP and UGT collections of genes. Genome-size estimation (420 Mb) was based on the total size of assembled scaffolds and corresponded to the value derived from the k-mer distribution of Illumina 100-bp PE reads (445 Mb) using Jellyfish (<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">45</a>). The organellar (mitochondrial and chloroplastic) genomes were filtered based on BLAST analysis against the melon organellar genome database and were excluded from the genome assembly. To collect the complete genomic catalog of the five enzyme families (squalene epoxidases, epoxide hydrolases, triterpene synthases, CYP450, and UGT), irrespective of their expression in the tissues studied, the genome assembly was used to identify all of the members of the families.</p></div><div id="sec-18" class="subsection"><h3>Data Availability.</h3><p id="p-55">The raw sequencing data were deposited in the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) Sequence Read Archive database as Bioproject PRJNA339375.</p></div><div id="sec-19" class="subsection"><h3>Specific Gene Family Expression Analysis.</h3><p id="p-56">The transcriptome assembly contigs annotated as CYP450, together with annotated CYP450 sequences from the melonomics database (<a href="https://melonomics.net/" class="in-nw">https://melonomics.net/</a>; version 3.5), combined with a comprehensive list of plant Cytochrome P450-dependent monooxygenase (CYP) sequences (<a id="xref-ref-38-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">38</a>, <a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">46</a>), were combined. This collection was used to search against the Moleculo long-reads using Basic Local Alignment Search Tool (BLASTN) (<a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>) to extract “CYP reads” (<em>E</em>-value cutoff of 0.001). The CYP reads were de novo-assembled using Celera Assembler 8.2 (<a id="xref-ref-43-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">43</a>) using default parameters and manually corrected as necessary into a <em>Siraitia</em> genome CYP450 database. These are presented in <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sd02.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>.</p><p id="p-57">Similarly, 158 UDP-glucosyltransferase sequences from the melon database (<a href="https://melonomics.net/" class="in-nw">https://melonomics.net/</a>; version 3.5) combined with the annotated UGT transcript contigs from the <em>Siraitia</em> transcript de novo assembly were searched against the TLSR library (via BLASTN, E-value&lt; 0.001). The “UGT reads” were also assembled using Celera Assembler 8.2 using default parameters and manually corrected as necessary into a <em>Siraitia</em> genome UGT database. These are presented in <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sd02.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>.</p><p id="p-58">In a similar manner the smaller gene families were also collected, searching the annotated melon database combined with the annotated de novo transcriptome assembly. These are presented in <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sd02.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>.</p><p id="p-59">GeneScan software (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>) was used for predicting the locations and exon–intron structures of genes in the CYP and UGT genomic assemblies. These were corrected manually, based on BLASTX comparisons of the genomic scaffolds, against the NCBI nr database. The predicted genes were annotated against the melon protein database (<a href="https://melonomics.net/" class="in-nw">https://melonomics.net/</a>; version 3.5; BLASTX) for extricating the genes of the five families.</p><p id="p-60">The expression estimation of the five family genes was carried out using the RNA-seq libraries. The nine single-end 100-bp libraries of 15-DAA, 34-DAA, 50-DAA, 77-DAA, 90-DAA, and 103-DAA fruit, stems, leaves, and roots) were mapped on the reference five-family genes using version 2.1.0 of bowtie2 (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">49</a>), and soap.coverage (version 2.7.7) was used for depth estimation of each gene in each sample. The expression data are presented in <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sd02.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>.</p></div><div id="sec-20" class="subsection"><h3>Phylogenetic and Cluster Analysis.</h3><p id="p-61">Alignments of protein sequences for the large phylogenetic analyses (UGT and CYP) were performed using the mafft program using default parameters (<a href="http://mafft.cbrc.jp/alignment/server/" class="in-nw">mafft.cbrc.jp/alignment/server/</a>). Phylogenetic trees were constructed based on a maximum likelihood framework using phyml software (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>) based on the LG matrix-based model (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>). The trees were graphically designed using Figtree version 1.4 (<a href="http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/software/figtree/" class="in-nw">tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/software/figtree/</a>). For the smaller phylogenetic analyses (SQE, EPH) the one-click phylogeny.fr program (<a href="http://phylogeny.lirmm.fr/phylo_cgi/index.cgi" class="in-nw">phylogeny.lirmm.fr/phylo_cgi/index.cgi</a>) was used (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>). Hierarchical clustering of genes was performed based on the expression values using Expander 7 software (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>) and the CLICK and Kmeans algorithms (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-21" class="subsection"><h3>Gene Expression and Functional Analysis.</h3><p id="p-62">Candidate genes were cloned using standard laboratory methods, or synthetic genes were prepared by Gen9Bio based on identified sequences.</p><div id="sec-22" class="subsection"><h4>Cucurbitadienol synthase.</h4><p id="p-63">The coding region fragment for CDS was synthesized by Gen9Bio to contain additional restriction sites (NotI and Cla) to allow its cloning in MCS1 of the yeast expression vector pESC-URA (Agilent) under the control of the GAL10 promoter and designated as pESC-CDS. The constructs were transformed into DH5α competent cells, and plasmid DNAs were prepared and used to transform the yeast strain GIL77 (gal2 hem3-6 erg7 ura3-167), which was kindly provided by M. Shibuya and Y. Ebizuka, Graduate School of Pharmaceutical Sciences, University of Tokyo, Tokyo (<a id="xref-ref-32-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>). Yeast GIL77 was maintained on YEPD medium supplemented with ergosterol (20 mg/mL) and Tween 80 (5 mg/mL). Transgenic tobacco plants expressing CDS were produced as follows. The full-length <em>CDS</em> was used as a template for a PCR with the forward and reverse primers (F-GGGGACAAGTTTGTACAAAAAAGCAGGCTATGTGGAGGTTAAAGGTCGGA and R-GGGGACCACTTTGTACAAGAAAGCTGGGTTTATTCAGTCAAAACCCGATGG), rendering it suitable for the Gateway system (Invitrogen). The resulting amplicon was cloned into the pENTR vector and subsequently cloned into the plant expression vector Pk7wg2.0. The resulting plasmid, Pk7wg2.0-CDS, was expressed in tobacco plants as described in Cohen et al. (<a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-23" class="subsection"><h4>Epoxide hydrolase.</h4><p id="p-64">The coding-region fragments for the candidate EPHs were synthesized by Gen9Bio to contain additional restriction sites (SalI and NheI) for their cloning into the MSC2 site of the pESC-CDS vector under the control of the GAL1 promoter. The resulting plasmids were designated pESC-CDS_EPH1, -2, or -3 and were then transformed in the yeast strain GIL77. Relative activity of EPH was calculated from the integrated peak area of di-hydroxycucurbitadienol and 24,25-epoxycucurbitadienol in the control and <em>EPH</em>-expressing yeast lines, as calculated by the MassHunter Qualitative analysis version B.06.00 using Find by Molecular Formula algorithm (Agilent Technologies, Inc.).</p></div><div id="sec-24" class="subsection"><h4>CYP450s.</h4><p id="p-65">The coding-region fragments for the candidate CYPs were synthesized by Gen9Bio, as above. The resulting plasmids were designated pESC-CDS_CYPXXX and were then transformed in the yeast strain BY4743_YHR072 (MATa/α his3Δ1/his3Δ1 leu2Δ0/leu2Δ0 LYS2/lys2Δ0 met15Δ0/MET15 ura3Δ0/ura3Δ0 kanMax::erg7/ERG7; Thermo). For the analysis of CYPs, yeast was transformed with pESC-HIS vector harboring the <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> NADPH-CYP reductase under GAL1 induction (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">56</a>). For the additional expression of <em>SgEPH3</em> in BY4743_YHR072 yeast expressing <em>SgCYP87D18</em>, <em>AtCPR</em>, and <em>SgCDS</em>, the <em>SgEPH3</em> was cloned in pESC-Leu. Yeast for this experiment were grown in the presence of the lanosterol synthase inhibitor R0 48–8072 (<a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">57</a>) (50 µg/mL) (Cayman Chemicals), added 4–10 h following galactose induction.</p></div><div id="sec-25" class="subsection"><h4>UGTs.</h4><p id="p-66">UGT sequences were designed to contain the NheI restriction site at 5′ to create fusion with His-tag and the NotI restriction site at 3′ following the stop codon. Synthetic DNA was cut with NheI and Not enzymes, subcloned into pET28a expression vector (Novagen), and sequenced for verification. For UGT functional expression, Arctic Express (Agilent Technologies, Inc.) bacteria, containing UGT genes in pET28a (Novagen) vector, were grown overnight in 5 mL of LB at 37 °C at 200 × <em>g</em>. Then, 20 mL of LB was inoculated with 0.8 mL of overnight culture and grown until OD<sub>600</sub> = 0.4. Next they were induced in 4 mM IPTG overnight at room temperature, cells were collected (5-min centrifugation at 10,000 × <em>g</em>), and the pellet was resuspended in 1.5 mL of 50 mM Tris⋅HCl, pH 7.0, 15% (vol/vol) glycerol, 0.1 mM EDTA, and 5 mM β-mercaptoethanol. After breaking the cells by five cycles of half-hour sonication/freeze in liquid nitrogen, insoluble material was removed by centrifugation for 1 h at 20,000 × <em>g</em>, and the soluble fractions were used for characterization of the enzymes. Proteins were stored at −20 °C until further analysis.</p></div></div><div id="sec-26" class="subsection"><h3>Heterologous Expression in Recombinant Yeast and Analysis.</h3><p id="p-67">Single transformed yeast colonies were incubated overnight in 3 mL synthetic complete medium without the appropriate selection markers at 30 °C and 220 × <em>g</em> and were then used for the inoculation of 20 mL medium. After 2% (wt/vol) galactose induction for 2 d, cells were collected by centrifugation, and the pellet was disrupted with 2.5 mL of hot 20% (wt/vol) potassium hydroxide and 50% (vol/vol) ethanol and extracted twice with a similar volume of <em>n</em>-hexane. The hexane extract was evaporated and resuspended in 1 mL methanol for the analysis of the cucurbitadienol product using a liquid chromatography-mass spectrometry (LC-MS) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix, Methods</em></a>). In the case of hydroxylation products, the extraction protocol was modified, and we used the commercial yeast lysis buffer Yeast Buster (Merck) for cell disruption and ethyl acetate for extraction.</p></div><div id="sec-27" class="subsection"><h3>Heterologous Expression in <em>E. coli</em>, UGT Enzyme Assays.</h3><p id="p-68">The mogroside substrates were dissolved to 1 mM in 50% DMSO. Enzyme assays were carried out in 50 mM Tris⋅HCl, pH 7.0, containing 5 mM β-mercaptoethanol, using up to 25 µL crude extract, 8 mM UDP-glucose, and 0.1 mM substrate in a final reaction volume of 100 µL. After overnight incubation at 37 °C, reactions were stopped by addition of 300 µL methanol, followed by brief vortexing. Subsequently, the extracts were centrifuged for 10 min at 20,000 × <em>g</em> and analyzed initially by LC-DAD (liquid chromatography with diode array detector); reactions showing evidence of new products were further analyzed by LC-MS (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Methods</em></a>). The amount of product was measured by the peak area in the LC-MS chromatogram and compared with the control with an enzyme preparation of <em>E. coli</em> harboring an empty vector.</p></div><div id="sec-28" class="subsection"><h3>Protein Modeling.</h3><p id="p-69">See <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1604828113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Methods</em></a>.</p></div></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-70">We thank Prof. Shibuta and Prof. Ebizuka for the generous gift of the yeast strain GIL77; Prof. Michael Court for the classification of the relevant UGTs; and Dr. Sariel Hubner for advice regarding the genome assembly. The mogroside standards of M4, M5, IM5, and M6 were supplied by Dr. Indra Prakash, Dr. Venkata Sai Prakash Chaturvedula, and Dr. Michael Brown from The Coca Cola Co. The research was partially supported by The Coca Cola Co.</p></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><h2>Footnotes</h2><ul class="pnas-footnotes"><li class="fn-equal" id="fn-3"><p id="p-3"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-fn-3-1">↵</a><sup>1</sup>M.I., R.D.-R., and S.C. contributed equally to this work.</p></li><li class="corresp" id="corresp-1"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-corresp-1-1">↵</a><sup>2</sup>To whom correspondence should be addressed. Email: <span class="em-link"><span class="em-addr">vcaris{at}agri.gov.il</span></span>.</li></ul><ul><li class="fn-other" id="fn-2"><p id="p-2">Author contributions: M.I., R.D.-R., S.C., A.D.-F., E.O., R.S., H.S.P., Y.T., Y.B., E.L., N.K., and A.S. designed research; M.I., R.D.-R., S.C., V.P., A.D.-F., E.O., S.F., G.T., N.B., S.S., M.P., R.S., H.G., A.H., and D.R.N. performed research; A.H. contributed new reagents/analytic tools; M.I., R.D.-R., S.C., V.P., A.D.-F., E.O., S.F., G.T., N.B., R.S., S.B.-D., H.G., A.H., D.R.N., H.S.P., Y.T., Y.B., E.L., N.K., and A.S. analyzed data; and M.I., R.D.-R., S.C., and A.S. wrote the paper.</p></li><li class="fn-conflict" id="fn-4"><p id="p-71">The authors declare no conflict of interest.</p></li><li class="fn-other" id="fn-5"><p id="p-72">This article is a PNAS Direct Submission.</p></li><li class="fn-other" id="fn-6"><p id="p-73">Data deposition: The raw sequencing data were deposited in the National Center for Biotechnology Information Sequence Read Archive database as Bioproject <a class="external-ref external-ref-type-ncbi:sra" href="/lookup/external-ref?link_type=ncbi%3Asra&amp;access_num=PRJNA339375&amp;atom=%2Fpnas%2F113%2F47%2FE7619.atom">PRJNA339375</a>.</p></li><li class="fn-supplementary-material" id="fn-7"><p id="p-74">This article contains supporting information online at <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1604828113/-/DCSupplemental</a>.</p></li></ul></div><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-end"></span></div><span id="related-urls"></span></div><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/113/47/E7619.abstract" class="hw-link hw-link-article-abstract" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View Abstract</a></div>  </div>

class="cit-ref-sprinkles cit-ref-sprinkles-openurl cit-ref-sprinkles-open-url"><span>OpenUrl</span></a><a href="/lookup/external-ref?access_num=10.1111/adb.12134&amp;link_type=DOI"
